571<div id="idm140456518425184" lang="en" class="tsec sec">

572<h2 class="head no_bottom_margin" id="idm140456518425184title">

573Abstract</h2>

574<!--article-meta-->

575<div>

576<div id="st1" class="sec sec-first">

577<h3 id="st1title">

578Background:</h3>

579<p id="__p1" class="p p-first-last">

580Numerous physical, psychological, and emotional benefits have been attributed to marijuana since its first reported use in 2,600 BC in a Chinese pharmacopoeia. The phytocannabinoids, cannabidiol (CBD), and delta-9-tetrahydrocannabinol (&#x00394;9-THC) are the most studied extracts from <em>

581cannabis sativa</em>

582 subspecies hemp and marijuana. CBD and &#x00394;9-THC interact uniquely with the endocannabinoid system (ECS). Through direct and indirect actions, intrinsic endocannabinoids and plant-based phytocannabinoids modulate and influence a variety of physiological systems influenced by the ECS.</p>

583</div>

584<div id="st2" class="sec">

585<h3 id="st2title">

586Methods:</h3>

587<p id="__p2" class="p p-first-last">

588In 1980, Cunha <em>

589et al</em>

590. reported anticonvulsant benefits in 7/8 subjects with medically uncontrolled epilepsy using marijuana extracts in a phase I clinical trial. Since then neurological applications have been the major focus of renewed research using medical marijuana and phytocannabinoid extracts.</p>

591</div>

592<div id="st3" class="sec">

593<h3 id="st3title">

594Results:</h3>

595<p id="__p3" class="p p-first-last">

596Recent neurological uses include adjunctive treatment for malignant brain tumors, Parkinson's disease, Alzheimer's disease, multiple sclerosis, neuropathic pain, and the childhood seizure disorders Lennox-Gastaut and Dravet syndromes. In addition, psychiatric and mood disorders, such as schizophrenia, anxiety, depression, addiction, postconcussion syndrome, and posttraumatic stress disorders are being studied using phytocannabinoids.</p>

597</div>

598<div id="st4" class="sec sec-last">

599<h3 id="st4title">

600Conclusions:</h3>

601<p id="__p4" class="p p-first-last">

602In this review we will provide animal and human research data on the current clinical neurological uses for CBD individually and in combination with &#x00394;9-THC. We will emphasize the neuroprotective, antiinflammatory, and immunomodulatory benefits of phytocannabinoids and their applications in various clinical syndromes.</p>

603</div>

604</div>

605<div class="sec">

606<strong class="kwd-title">

607Keywords: </strong>

608<span class="kwd-text">

609Cannabidiol, delta-9-tetrahydrocannabinol, endocannabinoid system, neurological disease, phytocannabinoids</span>

610</div>

611</div>

612<div id="sec1-1" class="tsec sec">

613<h2 class="head no_bottom_margin" id="sec1-1title">

614INTRODUCTION</h2>

615<p id="__p5" class="p p-first">

616Numerous physical, psychological, and emotional benefits have been attributed to marijuana since it was first reported in 2,600 BC (e.g., Chinese pharmacopoeia). The phytocannabinoids, cannabidiol (CBD), and delta-9-tetrahydrocannabinol (&#x00394;9-THC), the most studied extracts from the <em>

617cannabis sativa</em>

618 subspecies, include hemp and marijuana. Recently, it has been successfully utilized as an adjunctive treatment for malignant brain tumors, Parkinson's disease (PD), Alzheimer's disease (AD), multiple sclerosis (MS), neuropathic pain, and the childhood seizure disorders, Lennox-Gastaut and Dravet syndromes. In this review, we provide animal/human research data on the current clinical/neurological uses for CBD alone or with &#x00394;9-THC, emphasizing its neuroprotective, antiinflammatory, and immunomodulatory benefits when applied to various clinical situations.</p>

619<div id="sec2-1" class="sec">

620<h3 id="sec2-1title">

621Discovery of the endocannabinoid system</h3>

622<p id="__p6" class="p p-first">

623Cannabinoid receptor pharmacology began in the late 1960s when &#x00394;9-THC was isolated and synthesized and found to be the primary psychoactive constituent of marijuana.[<a href="#ref40" rid="ref40" class=" bibr popnode">

62440</a>

625,<a href="#ref80" rid="ref80" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_700126262">

62680</a>

627,<a href="#ref81" rid="ref81" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_700126251">

62881</a>

629,<a href="#ref105" rid="ref105" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_700445338">

630105</a>

631] The discovery in the early 1990s of specific membrane receptors for &#x00394;9-THC led to the identification of endogenous signaling system, now known as the endocannabinoid system (ECS). Shortly thereafter, the endogenous cannabinoids, <em>

632N</em>

633-arachidonoylethanolamine (anandamide) and 2-arachidonoylglycerol (2-AG), were identified. The ECS consists of two major types of endogenous G protein-coupled cannabinoid receptors (CB1 and CB2) located in the mammalian brain and throughout the central and peripheral nervous systems, including tissues associated with the immune system. CB1 and CB2 receptors can also co-exist in a variety of concentrations in the same locations.[<a href="#ref86" rid="ref86" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_700126209">

63486</a>

635] Both phytocannabinoids and endogenous cannabinoids function as retrograde messengers that provide feedback inhibition of both excitatory and inhibitory transmission in brain through the activation of presynaptic CB1 receptors. Manipulations of endocannabinoid degradative enzymes, CB1 and CB2 receptors, and their endogenous ligands have shown promise in modulating numerous processes associated with neurodegenerative diseases, cancer, epilepsy, and traumatic brain injury [<a href="/pmc/articles/PMC5938896/table/T1/" target="table" class="fig-table-link figpopup" rid-figpopup="T1" rid-ob="ob-T1" co-legend-rid="">

636<span>

637Table 1</span>

638</a>

639]. In addition, the ECS is known to influence neuroplasticity, apoptosis, excitotoxicity, neuroinflammation, and cerebrovascular breakdown associated with stroke and trauma.[<a href="#ref105" rid="ref105" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_700445330">

640105</a>

641]</p>

642<!--table ft1-->

643<!--table-wrap mode="anchored" t5-->

644<div class="table-wrap anchored whole_rhythm" id="T1">

645<h3>

646Table 1</h3>

647<!--caption a7-->

648<div class="caption">

649<p id="__p7">

650Conditions and Diseases in Which Activation of The ECS has Shown Benefit[<a href="#ref78" rid="ref78" class=" bibr popnode">

65178</a>

652]</p>

653</div>

654<div data-largeobj="" data-largeobj-link-rid="largeobj_idm140456513779344" class="figure">

663<div id="sec2-2" class="sec">

664<h3 id="sec2-2title">

665Phytocannabinoids CBD and &#x00394;9-THC</h3>

666<p id="__p8" class="p p-first">

667In addition to the phytocannabinoid &#x00394;9-THC, it is estimated that the <em>

668cannabis</em>

669 plant consists of over 400 chemical entities, of which more than 60 of them are phytocannabinoid compounds. Some of these compounds have been identified as acting uniquely on both CB1 and CB2 receptors separately and simultaneously, and/or to inhibit or activate receptor functions. CBD, like &#x00394;9-THC, is a major phytocannabinoid accounting for up to 40% of the plant's extract. CBD was first discovered in 1940 more than 20 years before &#x00394;9-THC.[<a href="#ref104" rid="ref104" class=" bibr popnode">

670104</a>

671,<a href="#ref105" rid="ref105" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_700445333">

672105</a>

673] Until recently, &#x00394;9-THC dominated cannabis research. All classes of phytocannabinoid compounds found in marijuana and hemp, including &#x00394;9-THC and CBD, are derived from various changes to base molecular structure of cannabigerol-type compounds [<a href="/pmc/articles/PMC5938896/figure/F1/" target="figure" class="fig-table-link figpopup" rid-figpopup="F1" rid-ob="ob-F1" co-legend-rid="lgnd_F1">

674<span>

675Figure 1</span>

676</a>

677].[<a href="#ref34" rid="ref34" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_700126296">

67834</a>

679,<a href="#ref49" rid="ref49" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_700126215">

68049</a>

681]</p>

682<!--fig ft0-->

683<!--fig mode=article f1-->

684<div class="fig iconblock whole_rhythm" id="F1" co-legend-rid="lgnd_F1">

685<a href="/pmc/articles/PMC5938896/figure/F1/" target="figure" rid-figpopup="F1" rid-ob="ob-F1">

686<!--fig/graphic|fig/alternatives/graphic mode="anchored" m1-->

687<div data-largeobj="" data-largeobj-link-rid="largeobj_idm140456514379472" class="figure">

688<img loading="lazy" class="fig-image" alt="An external file that holds a picture, illustration, etc.&#10;Object name is SNI-9-91-g002.jpg" title="An external file that holds a picture, illustration, etc.&#10;Object name is SNI-9-91-g002.jpg" src="/pmc/articles/PMC5938896/bin/SNI-9-91-g002.jpg" />

689</div>

690<div id="largeobj_idm140456514379472" class="largeobj-link align_right" style="display: none">

691<a target="object" rel="noopener" href="/pmc/articles/PMC5938896/figure/F1/?report=objectonly">

692Open in a separate window</a>

693</div>

694</a>

695<div class="icnblk_cntnt" id="lgnd_F1">

696<div>

697<a class="figpopup" href="/pmc/articles/PMC5938896/figure/F1/" target="figure" rid-figpopup="F1" rid-ob="ob-F1">

698Figure 1</a>

699</div>

700<!--caption a7-->

701<div class="caption">

702<p id="__p9">

703(a) &#x00394;9-THC and (b) cannabidiol (CBD) are biosynthesized as tetrahydrocannabonolic acid (THC-A) and cannabidolic acid (CBD-A) from a common precursor cannabigerolic acid (CBG). These phytocannabinoids in their natural acidic form are considered &#x0201c;inactive&#x0201d;. When cannabis grows, it produces THC-A and CBD-A, not &#x00394;9-THC and CBD. When cannabis is heated, such as through smoking, cooking, or vaporization, THC-A and CBD-A are decarboxylated into &#x00394;9-THC and CBD (i.e. &#x0201c;active&#x0201d; forms)[<a href="#ref44" rid="ref44" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_776967153">

70444</a>

705]</p>

706</div>

707</div>

708</div>

709</div>

710<div id="sec2-3" class="sec">

711<h3 id="sec2-3title">

712Cannabinoid receptors</h3>

713<p id="__p10" class="p p-first-last">

714Phytocannabinoid compounds and extracts can come from both hemp and marijuana subspecies, including CBD. CBD does not elicit the same psychoactive effects as seen with &#x00394;9-THC (i.e., users of CBD do not feel euphoric). The various psychoactive effects generally associated with &#x00394;9-THC are attributed to activation of the CB1 cannabinoid receptor found abundantly in the brain. CB1 receptors have the highest densities on the outflow nuclei of the basal ganglia, substantia nigra pars reticulata (SNr), and the internal and external segments of the globus pallidus (a portion of the brain that regulates voluntary movement).[<a href="#ref88" rid="ref88" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_700126282">

71588</a>

716] The hippocampus, particularly within the dentate gyrus, and cerebellum also have higher CB1 receptor densities. Very few CB1 receptors are found in the brainstem. These locations suggest CB1 receptor involvement in the modulation of memory, emotion, pain, and movement.[<a href="#ref37" rid="ref37" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_700126188">

71737</a>

718,<a href="#ref90" rid="ref90" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_700126220">

71990</a>

720] &#x00394;9-THC, which targets CB1 receptors, has been shown to reduce nociception in animal models of acute, visceral, inflammatory, and chronic pain. In patient studies with chronic pain and neuropathic pain, the use of marijuana or cannabinoid extracts produced positive and improved symptoms.[<a href="#ref52" rid="ref52" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_700126252">

72152</a>

722,<a href="#ref95" rid="ref95" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_700126211">

72395</a>

724]</p>

725</div>

726<div id="sec2-4" class="sec">

727<h3 id="sec2-4title">

728Activation of neuronal CB1 receptors</h3>

729<p id="__p11" class="p p-first">

730Activation of neuronal CB1 receptors results in inhibition of adenylyl cyclase and decreased neurotransmitter release through blockade of voltage-operated calcium channels.[<a href="#ref46" rid="ref46" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_700126283">

73146</a>

732,<a href="#ref65" rid="ref65" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_819929907">

73365</a>

734,<a href="#ref125" rid="ref125" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_700126180">

735125</a>

736] The activation of these signaling pathways by CB1 receptors and the high levels of these receptors on presynaptic terminals indicates that endocannabinoid stimulation of CB1 receptors suppresses neuronal excitability and inhibits neurotransmission. These effects have led to the study of phytocannabinoids for the treatment of epilepsy. Several pharmaceutical companies are attempting to develop synthetic high-affinity CB1 antagonists and inverse agonists as therapeutic drugs for diabetes, metabolic syndrome, and drug dependence.[<a href="#ref9" rid="ref9" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_700126257">

7379</a>

738,<a href="#ref42" rid="ref42" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_700126199">

73942</a>

740]</p>

741<div id="sec3-1" class="sec sec-last">

742<p>

743</p>

744<h4 id="sec3-1title" class="inline">

745The CB2 receptor </h4>

746<p id="__p12" class="p p-first-last">

747The CB2 receptor, unlike CB1, is not highly expressed in the central nervous system (CNS). Effects of &#x00394;9-THC on immune function have been attributed to the CB2 cannabinoid receptor interaction found predominantly in immune cells.[<a href="#ref54" rid="ref54" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_700126168">

74854</a>

749] CB2 receptors are distributed widely in the major tissues of immune cell production and regulation, including the spleen, tonsils, and thymus.[<a href="#ref89" rid="ref89" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_700126255">

75089</a>

751] These cell lines include B and T lymphocytes, natural killer cells, monocytes, macrophages, microglial cells, and mast cells. Like CB1 receptors, endocannabinoid stimulation inhibits neurotransmission of CB2 receptors.</p>

752</div>

753</div>

754<div id="sec2-5" class="sec">

755<h3 id="sec2-5title">

756Cultured microglial dells</h3>

757<p id="__p13" class="p p-first-last">

758A study of cultured microglial cells showed c-interferon and granulocyte macrophage-colony stimulating factor (GM-CSF), known as inflammatory response activators of microglial cell, were accompanied by significant CB2 receptor upregulation.[<a href="#ref73" rid="ref73" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_700126200">

75973</a>

760] This suggested that the CB2 receptors play an important role in microglial cell function in the CNS inflammatory response. Activation of CB2 has been implicated in several neurodegenerative diseases such as Huntington's (HD) and AD.[<a href="#ref21" rid="ref21" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_700126171">

76121</a>

762,<a href="#ref67" rid="ref67" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_819929906">

76367</a>

764,<a href="#ref98" rid="ref98" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_700126192">

76598</a>

766] Increased expression of CB2 in the brain was confirmed with CB2-selective positron emission tomography (PET) tracers in Alzheimer's mice models. This increased expression was concomitant with the formation of amyloid-beta plaques, suggesting a potential utility for CB2 PET tracers as a diagnostic modality for detecting the onset of neuroinflammation.</p>

767</div>

768<div id="sec2-6" class="sec">

769<h3 id="sec2-6title">

770Unique mechanisms of CBD</h3>

771<p id="__p14" class="p p-first">

772The interaction of CBD with CB2 receptors is more complex, but like &#x00394;9-THC, CBD is believed to reduce the inflammatory response. CBD's action with the CB2 receptor is just one of several pathways by which CBD can affect neuroinflammation [<a href="/pmc/articles/PMC5938896/table/T2/" target="table" class="fig-table-link figpopup" rid-figpopup="T2" rid-ob="ob-T2" co-legend-rid="">

773<span>

774Table 2</span>

775</a>

776]. Because both CBD and &#x00394;9-THC modulate the activity of G protein-coupled receptors associated with the endocannabinoid system, and CBD can function as a partial agonist and antagonizes &#x00394;9-THC, CBD at higher doses may counter to some extent the psychoactive effects of &#x00394;9-THC.[<a href="#ref77" rid="ref77" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_700126175">

77777</a>

778] Anecdotally, this effect is noted by many cannabis users who co-ingest CBD.</p>

779<!--table ft1-->

780<!--table-wrap mode="anchored" t5-->

781<div class="table-wrap anchored whole_rhythm" id="T2">

782<h3>

783Table 2</h3>

784<!--caption a7-->

785<div class="caption">

786<p id="__p15">

787Ongoing research indicates a wide range of cellular mechanisms associated with CBD and the ECS. Specific cellular targets include neurons, endothelial cells, oligodendrocytes and microglial cells [<a href="#ref12" rid="ref12" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_700126189">

78812</a>

789,<a href="#ref78" rid="ref78" class=" bibr popnode">

79078</a>

791,<a href="#ref92" rid="ref92" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_819929904">

79292</a>

793,<a href="#ref108" rid="ref108" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_700445328">

794108</a>

795]</p>

796</div>

797<div data-largeobj="" data-largeobj-link-rid="largeobj_idm140456506725008" class="figure">

798<img loading="lazy" class="fig-image" alt="An external file that holds a picture, illustration, etc.&#10;Object name is SNI-9-91-g003.jpg" title="An external file that holds a picture, illustration, etc.&#10;Object name is SNI-9-91-g003.jpg" src="/pmc/articles/PMC5938896/bin/SNI-9-91-g003.jpg" />

799</div>

800<div id="largeobj_idm140456506725008" class="largeobj-link align_right" style="display: none">

802Open in a separate window</a>

803</div>

804</div>

805</div>

806<div id="sec2-7" class="sec">

807<h3 id="sec2-7title">

808Molecular targets of CBD</h3>

809<p id="__p16" class="p p-first-last">

810Molecular targets of CBD, including cannabinoid and noncannabinoid receptors, enzymes, transporters, and cellular uptake proteins, help to explain CBD's low-binding affinity to both CB1 and CB2 cannabinoid receptors. In animal models, CBD has demonstrated an ability to attenuate brain damage associated with neurodegenerative and/or ischemic conditions outside the ECS. CBD appears to stimulate synaptic plasticity and facilitates neurogenesis that may explain its positive effects on attenuating psychotic, anxiety, and depressive behaviors. The mechanisms underlying these effects involve multiple cellular targets to elevate brain-derived neurotropic factor (BDNF) levels, reduce microglia activation, and decrease levels of proinflammatory mediators.[<a href="#ref82" rid="ref82" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_700126297">

81182</a>

812,<a href="#ref122" rid="ref122" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_700126273">

813122</a>

814,<a href="#ref123" rid="ref123" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_700126185">

815123</a>

816]</p>

817</div>

818<div id="sec2-8" class="sec">

819<h3 id="sec2-8title">

820Very low toxicity of CBD in humans</h3>

821<p id="__p17" class="p p-first-last">

822Unlike the psychoactive properties associated with &#x00394;9-THC, CBD has been shown to have very low toxicity in humans and in other species (see Safety section). Ingested and absorbed CBD is rapidly distributed, and due to its lipophilic nature can easily pass the blood&#x02013;brain barrier. The terminal half-life of CBD is about 9 h and is preferentially excreted in the urine as its free and glucuronide form.[<a href="#ref78" rid="ref78" class=" bibr popnode">

82378</a>

824]</p>

825</div>

826<div id="sec2-9" class="sec">

827<h3 id="sec2-9title">

828Research on the endocannabinoid system</h3>

829<p id="__p18" class="p p-first">

830Research on the ECS is fervently ongoing with wide-ranging discoveries. The roles of endogenous cannabinoid, phytocannabinoids, and synthetic pharmacological agents acting on the various elements of the ECS have a potential to affect a wide range of pathologies, including food intake disorders, chronic pain, emesis, insomnia, glaucoma, gliomas, involuntary motor disorders, stroke, and psychiatric conditions such as depression, autism, and schizophrenia.[<a href="#ref68" rid="ref68" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_700126162">

83168</a>

832] Research into ECS's role in the stress response has revealed a significant influence on the hypothalamic&#x02013;pituitary&#x02013;adrenal axis, the control of reproduction by modifying gonadotropin release, fertility, and sexual behavior.</p>

833<p id="__p19" class="p p-last">

834The remaining sections will focus on the ECS and the effects of the phytocannabinoids, CBD and &#x00394;9-THC, on neuroinflammation, neuroprotection, and their potential use in the treatment of specific neurological disorders including trauma involving the CNS.[<a href="#ref106" rid="ref106" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_700126178">

835106</a>

836] The cited research will include examples of clinical benefits of CBD alone and in combination with &#x00394;9-THC.[<a href="#ref12" rid="ref12" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_700126240">

83712</a>

838,<a href="#ref84" rid="ref84" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_700126275">

83984</a>

840]</p>

841</div>

842<div id="sec2-10" class="sec">

843<h3 id="sec2-10title">

844Neuroprotective benefits of phytocannabinoids</h3>

845<p id="__p20" class="p p-first-last">

846CBD research in animal models and humans has shown numerous therapeutic properties for brain function and protection, both by its effect on the ECS directly and by influencing endogenous cannabinoids. Broadly, CBD has demonstrated anxiolytic, antidepressant, neuroprotective antiinflammatory, and immunomodulatory benefits. CBD decreases the production of inflammatory cytokines, influences microglial cells to return to a ramified state, preserves cerebral circulation during ischemic events, and reduces vascular changes and neuroinflammation.[<a href="#ref12" rid="ref12" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_700126272">

84712</a>

848]</p>

849</div>

850<div id="sec2-11" class="sec">

851<h3 id="sec2-11title">

852Other effects of CBD</h3>

853<p id="__p21" class="p p-first-last">

854Other effects of CBD include the inhibition of calcium transport across membranes, the inhibition of anandamide uptake and enzymatic hydrolysis, and inhibition of inducible <em>

855NO synthase</em>

856 protein expression and nuclear factor (NF)-&#x003ba;B activation. CBD increases brain adenosine levels by reducing adenosine reuptake. Increased adenosine is associated with neuroprotection and decreased inflammation after brain trauma.[<a href="#ref7" rid="ref7" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_776967152">

8577</a>

858,<a href="#ref16" rid="ref16" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_700126270">

85916</a>

860] CBD is also known to exert vascular effects, producing vasodilation as well as hypotension that may hold promise as protectant against cerebrovascular damage associated with stroke.[<a href="#ref53" rid="ref53" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_700126230">

86153</a>

862,<a href="#ref105" rid="ref105" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_700445339">

863105</a>

864] CBD has several features that may be exploited for the treatment of AD, including the prevention of glutamate-induced excitotoxicity, reduction of proinflammatory mediators, and the ability to scavenge reactive oxygen species (ROS) and reduce lipid peroxidation.[<a href="#ref16" rid="ref16" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_700126198">

86516</a>

866,<a href="#ref32" rid="ref32" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_700126160">

86732</a>

868,<a href="#ref48" rid="ref48" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_700126190">

86948</a>

870,<a href="#ref59" rid="ref59" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_700126231">

87159</a>

872]</p>

873</div>

874<div id="sec2-12" class="sec">

875<h3 id="sec2-12title">

876Experimental <em>

877in vitro</em>

878 cannabinoid receptor interactions</h3>

879<p id="__p22" class="p p-first-last">

880Experimentally, <em>

881in vitro</em>

882, cannabinoid receptor interactions with CBD and &#x00394;9-THC, together and separately, have demonstrated neuronal protection from excitotoxicity, hypoxia, and glucose deprivation; <em>

883in vivo</em>

884, cannabinoids decrease hippocampal neuronal loss and infarct volume after cerebral ischemia, acute brain trauma, and induced excitotoxicity. These effects have been ascribed to inhibition of glutamate transmission, reduction of calcium influx, reduced microglial activation, and subsequent inhibition of noxious cascades, such as tumor necrosis factor-alpha generation and oxidative stress.[<a href="#ref92" rid="ref92" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_819929903">

88592</a>

886]</p>

887</div>

888<div id="sec2-13" class="sec">

889<h3 id="sec2-13title">

890Delta-9-THC</h3>

891<p id="__p23" class="p p-first-last">

892&#x00394;9-THC can mediate the effects of the neurotransmitter serotonin by decreasing 5-HT3 receptor neurotransmission. This can contribute to the pharmacological action to reduce nausea. This effect can be reversed at higher doses or with chronic use of &#x00394;9-THC.[<a href="#ref103" rid="ref103" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_700126260">

893103</a>

894,<a href="#ref117" rid="ref117" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_700126165">

895117</a>

896] Synthetic analogs of &#x00394;9-THC, nabilone (Cesamet, Valeant Pharmaceuticals North America) and dronabinol (Marinol-Solvay Pharmaceuticals), are prescribed for the suppression of the nausea and vomiting produced by chemotherapy.[<a href="#ref87" rid="ref87" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_700126228">

89787</a>

898] There is limited use for synthetic &#x00394;9-THC due to multiple side effects mediated through activation of CB1 on the CNS in this population.</p>

899</div>

900<div id="sec2-14" class="sec">

901<h3 id="sec2-14title">

902Brain neuroprotective effects of delta-9-THC</h3>

903<p id="__p24" class="p p-first-last">

904&#x00394;9-THC has also been shown to protect the brain from various neuronal insults and improve the symptoms of neurodegeneration in animal models of MS, PD, HD, amyotrophic lateral sclerosis (ALS), and AD.[<a href="#ref23" rid="ref23" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_700126182">

90523</a>

906,<a href="#ref35" rid="ref35" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_700126236">

90735</a>

908,<a href="#ref41" rid="ref41" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_700126210">

90941</a>

910] Like CBD, &#x00394;9-THC can offer non-ECS protection by direct effect on neuronal cells, and nonneuronal elements within the brain. Mechanisms include modulation of excitatory glutamatergic transmissions and synaptic plasticity, modulation of immune responses, the release of antiinflammatory mediators, modulation of excitability of <em>

911N</em>

912-methyl-D-aspartate receptors and its effect on gap junctions, calcium, and antioxidants.[<a href="#ref70" rid="ref70" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_700126291">

91370</a>

914]</p>

915</div>

916<div id="sec2-15" class="sec">

917<h3 id="sec2-15title">

918Neurodegenerative diseases</h3>

919<div id="sec3-2" class="sec sec-first-last">

920<p>

921</p>

922<h4 id="sec3-2title" class="inline">

923Overview </h4>

924<p id="__p25" class="p p-first-last">

925Neurodegenerative diseases include a large group of conditions associated with progressive neuronal loss leading to a variety of clinical manifestations. Histomorphological changes can include gliosis and proliferation of microglia along with aggregates of misfolded or aberrant proteins. The most common neurodegenerative conditions include AD, ALS, HD, Lewy body disease, and PD.</p>

926</div>

927</div>

928<div id="sec2-16" class="sec">

929<h3 id="sec2-16title">

930Numerous applications of CBD and delta-9-THC for neurodegenerative diseases</h3>

931<p id="__p26" class="p p-first-last">

932Numerous applications for CBD and &#x00394;9-THC for neurodegenerative diseases are being evaluated for both symptom relief and as treatments for the underlying pathologic changes of neuronal tissue. Both CBD and &#x00394;9-THC can function as agonist and antagonistic on various receptors in the ECS. In addition, a wide range of non-ECS receptors can be influenced by both endogenous and phytocannabinoids.[<a href="#ref31" rid="ref31" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_700126238">

93331</a>

934,<a href="#ref92" rid="ref92" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_819929902">

93592</a>

936,<a href="#ref116" rid="ref116" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_700126225">

937116</a>

938]</p>

939</div>

940<div id="sec2-17" class="sec">

941<h3 id="sec2-17title">

942Neuroprotection for AD</h3>

943<p id="__p27" class="p p-first-last">

944AD is characterized by enhanced beta-amyloid peptide deposition along with glial activation in senile plaques, selective neuronal loss, and cognitive deficits. Cannabinoids are neuroprotective against excitotoxicity <em>

945in vitro</em>

946 and in patients with acute brain damage. In human AD patients, cellular studies of senile plaques have shown expression of cannabinoid receptors CB1 and CB2, together with markers of microglial activation. Control CB1-positive neurons, however, are in greater numbers compared to AD areas of microglial activation. AD brains also have markedly decreased G-protein receptor coupling and CB1 receptor protein expression. Activated microglia cluster at senile plaques is generally believed to be responsible for the ongoing inflammatory process in the disease.</p>

947</div>

948<div id="sec2-18" class="sec">

949<h3 id="sec2-18title">

950Research with administered cannabinoids for AD</h3>

951<p id="__p28" class="p p-first-last">

952Research with administered cannabinoids for AD in animals has demonstrated CB1 agonism is able to prevent tau hyperphosphorylation in cultured neurons and antagonize cellular changes and behavioral consequences in &#x003b2;-amyloid-induced rodents. CB2 antagonists were protective in <em>

953in vivo</em>

954 experiments by downregulating reactive gliosis occurring in &#x003b2;-amyloid-injected animals. In addition, AD-induced microglial activation and loss of neurons was inhibited. AD-induced activation of cultured microglial cells, as judged by mitochondrial activity, cell morphology, and tumor necrosis factor release, is blunted by cannabinoid compounds.[<a href="#ref60" rid="ref60" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_776967154">

95560</a>

956,<a href="#ref92" rid="ref92" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_819929908">

95792</a>

958] Additionally, &#x00394;9-THC has been shown to reduce the agitation that is common in patients with severe AD.[<a href="#ref121" rid="ref121" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_700126195">

959121</a>

960]</p>

961</div>

962<div id="sec2-19" class="sec">

963<h3 id="sec2-19title">

964Cannabidiol</h3>

965<p id="__p29" class="p p-first">

966CBD is effective in an experimental model of Parkinsonism (6-hydroxydopamine-lesioned rats) by acting through antioxidant mechanisms independently of cannabinoid receptors.[<a href="#ref46" rid="ref46" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_700126294">

96746</a>

968] It attenuates PD-related dystonia, but not tremor,[<a href="#ref47" rid="ref47" class=" bibr popnode">

96947</a>

970] in agreement with a positive correlation between CBD levels measured in the putamen/globus pallidus of recreational users of cannabis.[<a href="#ref50" rid="ref50" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_700126263">

97150</a>

972]</p>

973<p id="__p30">

974In rats lesioned with 3-nitropropionic acid, a toxin inhibitor of the mitochondrial citric acid cycle resulting in a progressive locomotor deterioration resembling that of HD patients, CBD reduces rat striatal atrophy in a manner independent of the activation of cannabinoid adenosine A2A receptors.[<a href="#ref100" rid="ref100" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_700126156">

975100</a>

976] In contrast, CBD alone did not provide protection in rats lesioned with malonate.[<a href="#ref99" rid="ref99" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_700126287">

97799</a>

978] A clinical study from 1991 using 15 subjects with HD reported an average daily dose of CBD of 10 mg/kg/day per patient for 6 weeks was reported as safe but did not result on any significant symptom relief.[<a href="#ref51" rid="ref51" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_700126224">

97951</a>

980] The phytocannabinoid-based medicine Sativex&#x000ae;, a 1:1 combination of CBD and &#x00394;9-THC, has produced neuroprotective effects through a CB1- and CB2-mediated mechanism in a model of HD.[<a href="#ref115" rid="ref115" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_700126253">

981115</a>

982]</p>

983<p id="__p31">

984CBD, and to a lesser degree &#x00394;9-THC, can have both direct and indirect effects on isoforms of peroxisome proliferator-activated receptors (PPARs &#x003b1;, &#x003b2;, and &#x003b3;). Activation of PPAR, along with CB1 and CB2, mediates numerous analgesic, neuroprotective, neuronal function modulation, antiinflammatory, metabolic, antitumor, gastrointestinal, and cardiovascular effects, both in and outside the ECS. In addition, PPAR-&#x003b3; (gamma) agonists have been used in the treatment of hyperlipidemia and hyperglycemia. PPAR-&#x003b3; decreases the inflammatory response of many cardiovascular cells, particularly endothelial cells, thereby reducing atherosclerosis. Phytocannabinoids can increase the transcriptional activity of and exert effects that are inhibited by selective antagonists of PPAR-&#x003b3;, thus increasing production.[<a href="#ref33" rid="ref33" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_700126261">

98533</a>

986]</p>

987<p id="__p32" class="p p-last">

988CBD is further involved in the modulation of different receptors outside the ECS. The serotonin receptors have been implicated in the therapeutic effects of CBD. In a rat model, CBD was observed to stimulate hippocampal neurogenesis. Neuroprotective effects of CBD in hypoxic&#x02013;ischemic brain damage model involve adenosine A2 receptors. CBD activation of adenosine receptors can enhance adenosine signaling to mediate antiinflammatory and immunosuppressive effects. In a rat model of AD, CBD blunted the effects of reactive gliosis and subsequent &#x003b2;-amyloid-induced neurotoxicity.</p>

989</div>

990<div id="sec2-20" class="sec">

991<h3 id="sec2-20title">

992Delta-9-THC</h3>

993<p id="__p33" class="p p-first-last">

994In an animal model of AD, treatment with &#x00394;9-THC (3 mg/kg) once daily for 4 weeks with addition of a COX-2 inhibitor reduced the number of beta-amyloid plaques and degenerated neurons. &#x00394;9-THC has been used for AD symptom control. Treatment with 2.5 mg dronabinol (a synthetic analog of &#x00394;9-THC) daily for 2 weeks significantly improved the neuropsychiatric inventory total score for agitation and aberrant motor and nighttime behaviors.[<a href="#ref121" rid="ref121" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_700126219">

995121</a>

996]</p>

997</div>

998<div id="sec2-21" class="sec">

999<h3 id="sec2-21title">

1000Multiple sclerosis</h3>

1001<div id="sec3-3" class="sec sec-first-last">

1002<p>

1003</p>

1004<h4 id="sec3-3title" class="inline">

1005CBD and delta-9-THC </h4>

1006<p id="__p34" class="p p-first-last">

1007MS is an autoimmune disease that promotes demyelination of neurons and subsequent aberrant neuronal firing that contributes to spasticity and neuropathic pain. The pathologic changes of MS include neuroinflammation, excitotoxicity, demyelination, and neurodegeneration. These pathological features share similarities with other neurodegenerative conditions, including AD and cerebral ischemia. The combination of antiinflammatory, oligoprotective, and neuroprotective compounds that target the ECS may offer symptomatic and therapeutic treatment of MS.[<a href="#ref44" rid="ref44" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_776967151">

100844</a>

1009,<a href="#ref120" rid="ref120" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_700126293">

1010120</a>

1011]</p>

1012</div>

1013</div>

1014<div id="sec2-22" class="sec">

1015<h3 id="sec2-22title">

1016Use of cannabis-based medicine for neurodegenerative conditions</h3>

1017<p id="__p35" class="p p-first-last">

1018The use of cannabis-based medicine for the treatment of MS has a long history and its interaction with the ECS shares many of the same pathways of other neurodegenerative conditions.[<a href="#ref55" rid="ref55" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_700126281">

101955</a>

1020] In models of experimental MS, stimulation of CB1 and CB2 receptors has been shown to be beneficial against the inflammatory process, lending support to early findings showing that individuals with MS experience a reduction in the frequency of relapses when smoking marijuana.[<a href="#ref22" rid="ref22" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_700445335">

102122</a>

1022]</p>

1023</div>

1024<div id="sec2-23" class="sec">

1025<h3 id="sec2-23title">

1026American Academy of Neurology statement on medical marijuana</h3>

1027<p id="__p36" class="p p-first">

1028In 2014, the American Academy of Neurology (AAN) published a review article of 34 studies investigating the use of medical marijuana (as extracts, whole plants and synthetic phytocannabinoids) for possible neurological clinical benefits. They found strong support for symptoms of spasticity and spasticity-related pain, excluding neuropathic pain in the research using oral cannabis extracts. They reported inconclusive support for symptoms of urinary dysfunction, tremor, and dyskinesia. This study was subsequently used to form a consensus statement for their society. In the article they concluded their results based on the strength of the reported research [<a href="/pmc/articles/PMC5938896/table/T3/" target="table" class="fig-table-link figpopup" rid-figpopup="T3" rid-ob="ob-T3" co-legend-rid="">

1029<span>

1030Table 3</span>

1031</a>

1032].[<a href="#ref66" rid="ref66" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_700126191">

103366</a>

1034]</p>

1035<!--table ft1-->

1036<!--table-wrap mode="anchored" t5-->

1037<div class="table-wrap anchored whole_rhythm" id="T3">

1038<h3>

1039Table 3</h3>

1040<!--caption a7-->

1041<div class="caption">

1042<p id="__p37">

1043Conclusions from Subcommittee of the American Academy of Neurology (AAN) systematic review on medical marijuana in neurologic diseases published in 2014[<a href="#ref66" rid="ref66" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_700126241">

104466</a>

1045]</p>

1046</div>

1047<div data-largeobj="" data-largeobj-link-rid="largeobj_idm140456505751680" class="figure">

1048<img loading="lazy" class="fig-image" alt="An external file that holds a picture, illustration, etc.&#10;Object name is SNI-9-91-g004.jpg" title="An external file that holds a picture, illustration, etc.&#10;Object name is SNI-9-91-g004.jpg" src="/pmc/articles/PMC5938896/bin/SNI-9-91-g004.jpg" />

1049</div>
